<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Reptile</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p><b>Reptiles</b>, as most commonly defined, are the animals in the <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> <b>Reptilia</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a> grouping comprising all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropsid" title="Sauropsid">sauropsid</a> <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> except Aves (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">[1]</a></sup> Living reptiles comprise <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamates</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Rhynchocephalia" title="Rhynchocephalia">rhynchocephalians</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuatara</a>). As of March 2022, the <a href="/wiki/Reptile_Database" title="Reptile Database">Reptile Database</a> includes about 11,700 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In the traditional <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">Linnaean</a> classification system, birds are considered a separate class to reptiles. However, crocodilians are more closely related to birds than they are to other living reptiles, and so modern <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> classification systems include birds within Reptilia, redefining the term as a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>. Other cladistic definitions abandon the term reptile altogether in favor of the clade <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a>, which refers to all amniotes more closely related to modern reptiles than to mammals. The study of the traditional reptile <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">orders</a>, historically combined with that of modern <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, is called <a href="/wiki/Herpetology" title="Herpetology">herpetology</a>.
</p><p>The earliest known proto-reptiles originated around 312 million years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period, having evolved from advanced <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptiliomorph</a> tetrapods which became increasingly adapted to life on dry land. The earliest known <a href="/wiki/Eureptilia" title="Eureptilia">eureptile</a> ("true reptile") was <i><a href="/wiki/Hylonomus" title="Hylonomus">Hylonomus</a>,</i> a small and superficially lizard-like animal. Genetic and fossil data argues that the two largest lineages of reptiles, <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">Archosauromorpha</a> (crocodilians, birds, and kin) and <a href="/wiki/Lepidosauromorpha" title="Lepidosauromorpha">Lepidosauromorpha</a> (lizards, and kin), diverged near the end of the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-3">[3]</a></sup> In addition to the living reptiles, there are many diverse groups that are now <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinct</a>, in some cases due to <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinction events</a>. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a> wiped out the <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiosauria" title="Plesiosauria">plesiosaurs</a>, and all non-avian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinosaurs" title="Dinosaurs">dinosaurs</a> alongside many species of <a href="/wiki/Crocodyliformes" title="Crocodyliformes">crocodyliforms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamates</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur">mosasaurs</a>). Modern non-bird reptiles inhabit all the continents except <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.
</p><p>Reptiles are tetrapod <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a>, creatures that either have four limbs or, like snakes, are descended from four-limbed ancestors. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, reptiles do not have an aquatic larval stage. Most reptiles are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oviparous" title="Oviparous">oviparous</a>, although several species of squamates are <a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">viviparous</a>, as were some extinct aquatic clades<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S12_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-S12-4">[4]</a></sup> – the fetus develops within the mother, using a <a href="/wiki/Placentation#In_lizards_and_snakes" title="Placentation">(non-mammalian) placenta</a> rather than contained in an <a href="/wiki/Eggshell" title="Eggshell">eggshell</a>. As amniotes, reptile eggs are surrounded by membranes for protection and transport, which adapt them to reproduction on dry land. Many of the viviparous species feed their <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetuses</a> through various forms of placenta analogous to those of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, with some providing initial care for their hatchlings. <a href="/wiki/Neontology" title="Neontology">Extant</a> reptiles range in size from a tiny gecko, <i><a href="/wiki/Sphaerodactylus_ariasae" title="Sphaerodactylus ariasae">Sphaerodactylus ariasae</a></i>, which can grow up to 17 mm (0.7 in) to the <a href="/wiki/Saltwater_crocodile" title="Saltwater crocodile">saltwater crocodile</a>, <i>Crocodylus porosus</i>, which can reach over 6 m (19.7 ft) in length and weigh over 1,000 kg (2,200 lb).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_reptiles" title="List of reptiles">List of reptiles</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_history">Research history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Research history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Skull_roof" title="Skull roof">Skull roof</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reptile003d.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="13600" data-file-width="9604" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Reptile003d.jpg/220px-Reptile003d.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Reptile003d.jpg/330px-Reptile003d.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Reptile003d.jpg/440px-Reptile003d.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reptile003d.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reptiles, from <i>Nouveau Larousse Illustré</i>, 1897–1904, notice the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a> (below the crocodiles)</div></div></div>
<p>In the 13th century the category of <i>reptile</i> was recognized in Europe as consisting of a miscellany of egg-laying creatures, including "snakes, various fantastic monsters, lizards, assorted amphibians, and worms", as recorded by <a href="/wiki/Vincent_of_Beauvais" title="Vincent of Beauvais">Vincent of Beauvais</a> in his <i>Mirror of Nature</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
In the 18th century, the reptiles were, from the outset of classification, grouped with the <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, working from species-poor <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_adder" title="Common adder">common adder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grass_snake" title="Grass snake">grass snake</a> are often found hunting in water, included all reptiles and amphibians in <a href="/wiki/Class_(biology)" title="Class (biology)">class</a> "III – <a href="/wiki/Amphibia_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Amphibia in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">Amphibia</a>" in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systema_Natur%C3%A6" title="Systema Naturæ">Systema Naturæ</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linn1758_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linn1758-6">[6]</a></sup>
The terms <i>reptile</i> and <i>amphibian</i> were largely interchangeable, <i>reptile</i> (from Latin <i>repere</i>, 'to creep') being preferred by the French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Josephus_Nicolaus_Laurenti" title="Josephus Nicolaus Laurenti">Josephus Nicolaus Laurenti</a> was the first to formally use the term <i>Reptilia</i> for an expanded selection of reptiles and amphibians basically similar to that of Linnaeus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Today, the two groups are still commonly treated under the single heading <a href="/wiki/Herpetology" title="Herpetology">herpetology</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MosasaurDiscovery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="845" data-file-width="1836" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/MosasaurDiscovery.jpg/220px-MosasaurDiscovery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/MosasaurDiscovery.jpg/330px-MosasaurDiscovery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/MosasaurDiscovery.jpg/440px-MosasaurDiscovery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MosasaurDiscovery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Antediluvian monster", a <i><a href="/wiki/Mosasaurus" title="Mosasaurus">Mosasaurus</a></i> discovered in a <a href="/wiki/Maastricht" title="Maastricht">Maastricht</a> limestone quarry, 1770 (contemporary engraving)</div></div></div>
<p>It was not until the beginning of the 19th century that it became clear that reptiles and amphibians are, in fact, quite different animals, and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Andr%C3%A9_Latreille" title="Pierre André Latreille">Pierre André Latreille</a> erected the class <i>Batracia</i> (1825) for the latter, dividing the <a href="/wiki/Tetrapod" title="Tetrapod">tetrapods</a> into the four familiar classes of reptiles, amphibians, birds, and mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The British anatomist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a> made Latreille's definition popular and, together with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a>, expanded Reptilia to include the various fossil "<a href="/wiki/Antediluvian" title="Antediluvian">antediluvian</a> monsters", including <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and the mammal-like (<a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsid</a>) <i><a href="/wiki/Dicynodon" title="Dicynodon">Dicynodon</a></i> he helped describe. This was not the only possible classification scheme: In the Hunterian lectures delivered at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons_of_England" title="Royal College of Surgeons of England">Royal College of Surgeons</a> in 1863, Huxley grouped the vertebrates into <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, sauroids, and ichthyoids (the latter containing the fishes and amphibians). He subsequently proposed the names of <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichthyopsida" title="Ichthyopsida">Ichthyopsida</a> for the latter two groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In 1866, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Haeckel</a> demonstrated that vertebrates could be divided based on their reproductive strategies, and that reptiles, birds, and mammals were united by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniotic_egg" title="Amniotic egg">amniotic egg</a>.
</p><p>The terms <i>Sauropsida</i> ('lizard faces') and <i><a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">Theropsida</a></i> ('beast faces') were used again in 1916 by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Stephen_Goodrich" title="Edwin Stephen Goodrich">E.S. Goodrich</a> to distinguish between lizards, birds, and their relatives on the one hand (Sauropsida) and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> and their extinct relatives (Theropsida) on the other. Goodrich supported this division by the nature of the hearts and blood vessels in each group, and other features, such as the structure of the forebrain. According to Goodrich, both lineages evolved from an earlier stem group, Protosauria ("first lizards") in which he included some animals today considered <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptile-like amphibians</a>, as well as early reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goodrich1916_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-goodrich1916-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1956, <a href="/wiki/D._M._S._Watson" title="D. M. S. Watson">D.M.S. Watson</a> observed that the first two groups diverged very early in reptilian history, so he divided Goodrich's Protosauria between them. He also reinterpreted Sauropsida and Theropsida to exclude birds and mammals, respectively. Thus his Sauropsida included <a href="/wiki/Procolophonia" title="Procolophonia">Procolophonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eosuchia" title="Eosuchia">Eosuchia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millerettid" title="Millerettid">Millerosauria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">Chelonia</a> (turtles), <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">Squamata</a> (lizards and snakes), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphenodontia" title="Sphenodontia">Rhynchocephalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">Crocodilia</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Thecodontia" title="Thecodontia">thecodonts</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">Archosauria</a>), non-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/avian" title="wikt:avian">avian</a> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia">sauropterygians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watson1956_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-watson1956-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 19th century, a number of definitions of Reptilia were offered. The traits listed by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lydekker" title="Richard Lydekker">Lydekker</a> in 1896, for example, include a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occipital_condyle" title="Occipital condyle">occipital condyle</a>, a jaw joint formed by the <a href="/wiki/Quadrate_bone" title="Quadrate bone">quadrate</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Articular" title="Articular">articular</a> bones, and certain characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Vertebral_column" title="Vertebral column">vertebrae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The animals singled out by these formulations, the <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniotes</a> other than the mammals and the birds, are still those considered reptiles today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tudge_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-tudge-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3375" data-file-width="4575" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg/220px-Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg/330px-Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg/440px-Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captorhinus_aguti_p.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first reptiles had an <a href="/wiki/Anapsid" title="Anapsid">anapsid</a> type of <a href="/wiki/Skull_roof" title="Skull roof">skull roof</a>, as seen in the <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> genus <i><a href="/wiki/Captorhinus" title="Captorhinus">Captorhinus</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The synapsid/sauropsid division supplemented another approach, one that split the reptiles into four subclasses based on the number and position of <a href="/wiki/Skull#Temporal_fenestra" title="Skull">temporal fenestrae</a>, openings in the sides of the skull behind the eyes. This classification was initiated by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fairfield_Osborn" title="Henry Fairfield Osborn">Henry Fairfield Osborn</a> and elaborated and made popular by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Romer" title="Alfred Romer">Romer</a>'s classic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_Paleontology_(Romer)" title="Vertebrate Paleontology (Romer)">Vertebrate Paleontology</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-romer1933_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-romer1933-16">[16]</a></sup> Those four subclasses were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Anapsid" title="Anapsid">Anapsida</a> – no fenestrae – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cotylosaur" title="Cotylosaur">cotylosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">Chelonia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a> and relatives)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qualifier_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-qualifier-17">[note 1]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">Synapsida</a> – one low fenestra – <a href="/wiki/Pelycosaur" title="Pelycosaur">pelycosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Therapsid" title="Therapsid">therapsids</a> (the '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammal-like_reptiles" title="Mammal-like reptiles">mammal-like reptiles</a>')</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euryapsida" title="Euryapsida">Euryapsida</a> – one high fenestra (above the postorbital and squamosal) – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protorosaur" title="Protorosaur">protorosaurs</a> (small, early lizard-like reptiles) and the marine <a href="/wiki/Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia">sauropterygians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichthyosaurs" title="Ichthyosaurs">ichthyosaurs</a>, the latter called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parapsida" title="Parapsida">Parapsida</a> in Osborn's work.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">Diapsida</a> – two fenestrae – most reptiles, including <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian">crocodilians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Varanodon1DB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Varanodon1DB.jpg/220px-Varanodon1DB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Varanodon1DB.jpg/330px-Varanodon1DB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Varanodon1DB.jpg/440px-Varanodon1DB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Varanodon1DB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phylogenetic classifications group the traditional "mammal-like reptiles", like this <i><a href="/wiki/Varanodon" title="Varanodon">Varanodon</a></i>, with other synapsids, not with extant reptiles</div></div></div>
<p>The composition of Euryapsida was uncertain. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ichthyosaurs" title="Ichthyosaurs">Ichthyosaurs</a> were, at times, considered to have arisen independently of the other euryapsids, and given the older name Parapsida. Parapsida was later discarded as a group for the most part (ichthyosaurs being classified as <i><a href="/wiki/Incertae_sedis" title="Incertae sedis">incertae sedis</a></i> or with Euryapsida). However, four (or three if Euryapsida is merged into Diapsida) subclasses remained more or less universal for non-specialist work throughout the 20th century. It has largely been abandoned by recent researchers: In particular, the anapsid condition has been found to occur so variably among unrelated groups that it is not now considered a useful distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogenetics_and_modern_definition">Phylogenetics and modern definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Phylogenetics and modern definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the early 21st century, vertebrate paleontologists were beginning to adopt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> taxonomy, in which all groups are defined in such a way as to be <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">monophyletic</a>; that is, groups which include all descendants of a particular ancestor. The reptiles as historically defined are <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a>, since they exclude both birds and mammals. These respectively evolved from dinosaurs and from early therapsids, which were both traditionally called reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brysse2008WeirdWondersToStemLineages_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brysse2008WeirdWondersToStemLineages-19">[18]</a></sup> Birds are more closely related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian">crocodilians</a> than the latter are to the rest of extant reptiles. <a href="/wiki/Colin_Tudge" title="Colin Tudge">Colin Tudge</a> wrote:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Mammals are a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>, and therefore the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_nomenclature" title="Phylogenetic nomenclature">cladists</a> are happy to acknowledge the traditional taxon <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">Mammalia</a>; and birds, too, are a clade, universally ascribed to the formal taxon <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">Aves</a>. Mammalia and Aves are, in fact, subclades within the grand clade of the Amniota. But the traditional class Reptilia is not a clade. It is just a section of the clade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amniota" title="Amniota">Amniota</a>: the section that is left after the Mammalia and Aves have been hived off. It cannot be defined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapomorphy" title="Synapomorphy">synapomorphies</a>, as is the proper way. Instead, it is defined by a combination of the features it has and the features it lacks: reptiles are the amniotes that lack fur or feathers. At best, the cladists suggest, we could say that the traditional Reptilia are 'non-avian, non-mammalian amniotes'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tudge_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-tudge-14">[14]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Despite the early proposals for replacing the paraphyletic Reptilia with a monophyletic <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a>, which includes birds, that term was never adopted widely or, when it was, was not applied consistently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg/220px-Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg/330px-Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg/440px-Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bearded_Dragon_Skeleton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bearded dragon (<a href="/wiki/Pogona" title="Pogona">pogona</a>) skeleton on display at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Osteology" title="Museum of Osteology">Museum of Osteology</a></div></div></div>
<p>When Sauropsida was used, it often had the same content or even the same definition as Reptilia. In 1988, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Gauthier" title="Jacques Gauthier">Jacques Gauthier</a> proposed a <a href="/wiki/Cladistics" title="Cladistics">cladistic</a> definition of Reptilia as a monophyletic node-based <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">crown group</a> containing turtles, lizards and snakes, crocodilians, and birds, their common ancestor and all its descendants. While Gauthier's definition was close to the modern consensus, nonetheless, it became considered inadequate because the actual relationship of turtles to other reptiles was not yet well understood at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup> Major revisions since have included the reassignment of synapsids as non-reptiles, and classification of turtles as diapsids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>A variety of other definitions were proposed by other scientists in the years following Gauthier's paper. The first such new definition, which attempted to adhere to the standards of the <a href="/wiki/PhyloCode" title="PhyloCode">PhyloCode</a>, was published by Modesto and Anderson in 2004. Modesto and Anderson reviewed the many previous definitions and proposed a modified definition, which they intended to retain most traditional content of the group while keeping it stable and monophyletic. They defined Reptilia as all amniotes closer to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lacerta_agilis" title="Lacerta agilis">Lacerta agilis</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crocodylus_niloticus" title="Crocodylus niloticus">Crocodylus niloticus</a></i> than to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>. This stem-based definition is equivalent to the more common definition of Sauropsida, which Modesto and Anderson synonymized with Reptilia, since the latter is better known and more frequently used. Unlike most previous definitions of Reptilia, however, Modesto and Anderson's definition includes birds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup> as they are within the clade that includes both lizards and crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_reptiles" title="List of reptiles">List of reptiles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_snakes" title="List of snakes">List of snakes</a></div>
<p>General classification of extinct and living reptiles, focusing on major groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2005-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2014_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2014-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>Reptilia</b>/<b><a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a></b>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phylogeny">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cladistics#Cladograms" title="Cladistics">cladogram</a> presented here illustrates the "family tree" of reptiles, and follows a simplified version of the relationships found by M.S. Lee, in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scaffold2013_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-scaffold2013-23">[22]</a></sup> All <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">genetic</a> studies have supported the hypothesis that turtles are diapsids; some have placed turtles within Archosauromorpha,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scaffold2013_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-scaffold2013-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannen_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mannen-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zardoya_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zardoya-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iwabe_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iwabe-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roos_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roos-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katsu_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Katsu-28">[27]</a></sup> though a few have recovered turtles as Lepidosauromorpha instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> The cladogram below used a combination of genetic (molecular) and fossil (morphological) data to obtain its results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scaffold2013_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-scaffold2013-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r984819063">.mw-parser-output table.clade{border-spacing:0;margin:0;font-size:100%;line-height:100%;border-collapse:separate;width:auto}.mw-parser-output table.clade table.clade{width:100%;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label{width:0.7em;padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;border-bottom:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width{overflow:hidden;text-overflow:ellipsis}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-fixed-width:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.first{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-label.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel{padding:0 0.15em;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;border-left:1px solid;white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel:hover{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.last{border-left:none;border-right:none}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-slabel.reverse{border-left:none;border-right:1px solid}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar{vertical-align:middle;text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-bar.reverse{text-align:right;position:relative}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf{border:0;padding:0;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leafR{border:0;padding:0;text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade td.clade-leaf.reverse{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkA{background-color:yellow}.mw-parser-output table.clade:hover span.linkB{background-color:green}</style>

</td></tr>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_position_of_turtles">The position of turtles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The position of turtles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The placement of turtles has historically been highly variable. Classically, turtles were considered to be related to the primitive anapsid reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30">[29]</a></sup> Molecular work has usually placed turtles within the diapsids. As of 2013, three turtle genomes have been sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2013_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert2013-31">[30]</a></sup> The results place turtles as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_clade" title="Sister clade">sister clade</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a>, the group that includes crocodiles, dinosaurs, and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> However, in their comparative analysis of the timing of <a href="/wiki/Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis">organogenesis</a>, Werneburg and Sánchez-Villagra (2009) found support for the hypothesis that turtles belong to a separate clade within <a href="/wiki/Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida">Sauropsida</a>, outside the <a href="/wiki/Sauria" title="Sauria">saurian</a> clade altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Werneburg_and_Sánchez-Villagra_2009_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Werneburg_and_Sánchez-Villagra_2009-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_reptiles" title="Evolution of reptiles">Evolution of reptiles</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_reptiles">Origin of the reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Origin of the reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hylonomus_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/220px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/330px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/440px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hylonomus_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early reptile <i><a href="/wiki/Hylonomus" title="Hylonomus">Hylonomus</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/220px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/330px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/440px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mesozoic scene showing typical reptilian megafauna: <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europasaurus_holgeri" title="Europasaurus holgeri">Europasaurus holgeri</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iguanodont" title="Iguanodont">iguanodonts</a>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeopteryx_lithographica" title="Archaeopteryx lithographica">Archaeopteryx lithographica</a></i> perched on the foreground tree stump</div></div></div>
<p>The origin of the reptiles lies about 310–320 million years ago, in the steaming swamps of the late <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> period, when the first reptiles evolved from advanced <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptiliomorphs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laurin_95_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laurin_95-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest known animal that may have been an <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniote</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/Casineria" title="Casineria">Casineria</a></i> (though it may have been a <a href="/wiki/Temnospondyli" title="Temnospondyli">temnospondyl</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> A series of footprints from the fossil strata of <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> dated to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7015994064400000000♠"></span>315 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaannum" title="Megaannum">Ma</a></span> show typical reptilian toes and imprints of scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> These tracks are attributed to <i><a href="/wiki/Hylonomus" title="Hylonomus">Hylonomus</a></i>, the oldest unquestionable reptile known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
It was a small, lizard-like animal, about 20 to 30 centimetres (7.9 to 11.8 in) long, with numerous sharp teeth indicating an insectivorous diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoDP_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoDP-40">[39]</a></sup> Other examples include <i><a href="/wiki/Westlothiana" title="Westlothiana">Westlothiana</a></i> (for the moment considered a <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptiliomorph</a> rather than a true <a href="/wiki/Amniote" title="Amniote">amniote</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Paleothyris" title="Paleothyris">Paleothyris</a></i>, both of similar build and presumably similar habit.
</p><p>However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsaur" title="Microsaur">microsaurs</a> have been at times considered true reptiles, so an earlier origin is possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_reptiles">Rise of the reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Rise of the reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest amniotes, including stem-reptiles (those amniotes closer to modern reptiles than to mammals), were largely overshadowed by larger stem-tetrapods, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Cochleosaurus" title="Cochleosaurus">Cochleosaurus</a></i>, and remained a small, inconspicuous part of the fauna until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse">Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-43">[42]</a></sup> This sudden collapse affected several large groups. Primitive tetrapods were particularly devastated, while stem-reptiles fared better, being ecologically adapted to the drier conditions that followed. Primitive tetrapods, like modern amphibians, need to return to water to lay eggs; in contrast, amniotes, like modern reptiles – whose eggs possess a shell that allows them to be laid on land – were better adapted to the new conditions. Amniotes acquired new niches at a faster rate than before the collapse and at a much faster rate than primitive tetrapods. They acquired new feeding strategies including herbivory and carnivory, previously only having been insectivores and piscivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-43">[42]</a></sup> From this point forward, reptiles dominated communities and had a greater diversity than primitive tetrapods, setting the stage for the Mesozoic (known as the Age of Reptiles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates-44">[43]</a></sup> One of the best known early stem-reptiles is <i><a href="/wiki/Mesosaurus" title="Mesosaurus">Mesosaurus</a></i>, a genus from the <a href="/wiki/Cisuralian" title="Cisuralian">Early Permian</a> that had returned to water, feeding on fish.
</p><p>A 2021 examination of reptile diversity in the Carboniferous and Permian suggests a much higher degree of diversity than previously thought, comparable or even exceeding that of synapsids. Thus, the "First Age of Reptiles" was proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Anapsids.2C_synapsids.2C_diapsids.2C_and_sauropsids"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Anapsids,_synapsids,_diapsids,_and_sauropsids">Anapsids, synapsids, diapsids, and sauropsids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Anapsids, synapsids, diapsids, and sauropsids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skull_comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="568" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/170px-Skull_comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/255px-Skull_comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/340px-Skull_comparison.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skull_comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>A</b> = Anapsid,<br/> <b>B</b> = Synapsid,<br/> <b>C</b> = Diapsid</div></div></div>
<p>It was traditionally assumed that the first reptiles retained an <a href="/wiki/Anapsid" title="Anapsid">anapsid</a> skull inherited from their ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coven_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coven-45">[44]</a></sup> This type of skull has a <a href="/wiki/Skull_roof" title="Skull roof">skull roof</a> with only holes for the nostrils, eyes and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pineal_eye" title="Pineal eye">pineal eye</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30">[29]</a></sup> The discoveries of <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">synapsid</a>-like openings (see below) in the skull roof of the skulls of several members of <a href="/wiki/Parareptilia" title="Parareptilia">Parareptilia</a> (the clade containing most of the amniotes traditionally referred to as "anapsids"), including <a href="/wiki/Lanthanosuchoidea" title="Lanthanosuchoidea">lanthanosuchoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millerettidae" title="Millerettidae">millerettids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolosauridae" title="Bolosauridae">bolosaurids</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Nycteroleteridae" title="Nycteroleteridae">nycteroleterids</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Procolophonoidea" title="Procolophonoidea">procolophonoids</a> and at least some <a href="/wiki/Mesosaur" title="Mesosaur">mesosaurs</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TsujiMuller2009FR_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-TsujiMuller2009FR-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PineiroetalCRP2012_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-PineiroetalCRP2012-48">[47]</a></sup> made it more ambiguous and it's currently uncertain whether the ancestral amniote had an anapsid-like or synapsid-like skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PineiroetalCRP2012_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-PineiroetalCRP2012-48">[47]</a></sup> These animals are traditionally referred to as "anapsids", and form a <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> basic stock from which other groups evolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20">[19]</a></sup> Very shortly after the first amniotes appeared, a lineage called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synapsida" title="Synapsida">Synapsida</a> split off; this group was characterized by a temporal opening in the skull behind each eye to give room for the jaw muscle to move. These are the "mammal-like amniotes", or stem-mammals, that later gave rise to the true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammals" title="Mammals">mammals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Soon after, another group evolved a similar trait, this time with a double opening behind each eye, earning them the name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diapsida" title="Diapsida">Diapsida</a> ("two arches").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coven_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coven-45">[44]</a></sup> The function of the holes in these groups was to lighten the skull and give room for the jaw muscles to move, allowing for a more powerful bite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Turtles have been traditionally believed to be surviving parareptiles, on the basis of their anapsid skull structure, which was assumed to be primitive trait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> The rationale for this classification has been disputed, with some arguing that turtles are diapsids that evolved anapsid skulls in order to improve their armor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laurin_95_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laurin_95-34">[33]</a></sup> Later morphological <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> studies with this in mind placed turtles firmly within Diapsida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rieppel_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rieppel-51">[50]</a></sup> All <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular</a> studies have strongly upheld the placement of turtles within diapsids, most commonly as a sister group to extant <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zardoya_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zardoya-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iwabe_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iwabe-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roos_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roos-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Katsu_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Katsu-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permian_reptiles">Permian reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Permian reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the close of the <a href="/wiki/Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a>, the amniotes became the dominant tetrapod fauna. While primitive, terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha">reptiliomorphs</a> still existed, the synapsid amniotes evolved the first truly terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> (giant animals) in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelycosaurs" title="Pelycosaurs">pelycosaurs</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Edaphosaurus" title="Edaphosaurus">Edaphosaurus</a></i> and the carnivorous <i><a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">Dimetrodon</a></i>. In the mid-Permian period, the climate became drier, resulting in a change of fauna: The pelycosaurs were replaced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapsids" title="Therapsids">therapsids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2001_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2001-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The parareptiles, whose massive <a href="/wiki/Skull_roof" title="Skull roof">skull roofs</a> had no postorbital holes, continued and flourished throughout the Permian. The <a href="/wiki/Pareiasaur" title="Pareiasaur">pareiasaurian</a> parareptiles reached giant proportions in the late Permian, eventually disappearing at the close of the period (the turtles being possible survivors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2001_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2001-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in the period, the modern reptiles, or <a href="/wiki/Sauria" title="Sauria">crown-group reptiles</a>, evolved and split into two main lineages: the <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">Archosauromorpha</a> (forebears of <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Lepidosauromorpha" title="Lepidosauromorpha">Lepidosauromorpha</a> (predecessors of modern <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuataras</a>). Both groups remained lizard-like and relatively small and inconspicuous during the Permian.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesozoic_reptiles">Mesozoic reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mesozoic reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The close of the Permian saw the greatest mass extinction known (see the <a href="/wiki/Permian%E2%80%93Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>), an event prolonged by the combination of two or more distinct extinction pulses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-53">[52]</a></sup> Most of the earlier parareptile and synapsid megafauna disappeared, being replaced by the true reptiles, particularly <a href="/wiki/Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha">archosauromorphs</a>. These were characterized by elongated hind legs and an erect pose, the early forms looking somewhat like long-legged crocodiles. The <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a> became the dominant group during the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> period, though it took 30 million years before their diversity was as great as the animals that lived in the Permian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-53">[52]</a></sup> Archosaurs developed into the well-known <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaurs</a>, as well as the ancestors of <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>. Since reptiles, first <a href="/wiki/Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia">rauisuchians</a> and then dinosaurs, dominated the Mesozoic era, the interval is popularly known as the "Age of Reptiles". The dinosaurs also developed smaller forms, including the feather-bearing smaller <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropods</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period, these gave rise to the first true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divergence_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-divergence-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a> to Archosauromorpha is <a href="/wiki/Lepidosauromorpha" title="Lepidosauromorpha">Lepidosauromorpha</a>, containing <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuatara" title="Tuatara">tuataras</a>, as well as their fossil relatives. Lepidosauromorpha contained at least one major group of the Mesozoic sea reptiles: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosasaurs" title="Mosasaurs">mosasaurs</a>, which lived during the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period. The phylogenetic placement of other main groups of fossil sea reptiles – the <a href="/wiki/Ichthyopterygia" title="Ichthyopterygia">ichthyopterygians</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur">ichthyosaurs</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia">sauropterygians</a>, which evolved in the early Triassic – is more controversial. Different authors linked these groups either to lepidosauromorphs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-1">[1]</a></sup> or to archosauromorphs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> and ichthyopterygians were also argued to be diapsids that did not belong to the least inclusive clade containing lepidosauromorphs and archosauromorphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cenozoic_reptiles">Cenozoic reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cenozoic reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="567" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/220px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/330px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/440px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varanus_priscus" title="Varanus priscus">Varanus priscus</a></i> was a giant carnivorous <a href="/wiki/Goanna" title="Goanna">goanna</a> lizard, perhaps as long as 7 metres and weighing up to 1940 kilograms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Champsosaurus_natator.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1591" data-file-width="4196" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/220px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/330px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/440px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Champsosaurus_natator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skeleton of <i><a href="/wiki/Champsosaurus" title="Champsosaurus">Champsosaurus</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Choristodere" title="Choristodere">choristodere</a>, the latest surviving order of extinct reptiles. The last known choristoderes are known from the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, around 11.3 million years ago</div></div></div>
<p>The close of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> period saw the demise of the Mesozoic era reptilian megafauna (see the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, also known as K-T extinction event). Of the large marine reptiles, only <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a> were left; and of the non-marine large reptiles, only the semi-aquatic <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodiles</a> and broadly similar <a href="/wiki/Choristodera" title="Choristodera">choristoderes</a> survived the extinction, with last members of the latter, the lizard-like <i><a href="/wiki/Lazarussuchus" title="Lazarussuchus">Lazarussuchus</a></i>, becoming extinct in the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> Of the great host of dinosaurs dominating the Mesozoic, only the small beaked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a> survived. This dramatic extinction pattern at the end of the Mesozoic led into the Cenozoic. Mammals and birds filled the empty niches left behind by the reptilian megafauna and, while reptile diversification slowed, bird and mammal diversification took an exponential turn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-SahneyBentonFerry2010LinksDiversityVertebrates-44">[43]</a></sup> However, reptiles were still important components of the megafauna, particularly in the form of large and giant <a href="/wiki/Tortoise" title="Tortoise">tortoises</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cione_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cione-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the extinction of most archosaur and marine reptile lines by the end of the Cretaceous, reptile diversification continued throughout the Cenozoic. <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">Squamates</a> took a massive hit during the K–Pg event, only recovering ten million years after it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBG12_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-LBG12-63">[62]</a></sup> but they underwent a great radiation event once they recovered, and today squamates make up the majority of living reptiles (&gt; 95%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Reptile_Database_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Reptile_Database-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Approximately 10,000 extant species of traditional reptiles are known, with birds adding about 10,000 more, almost twice the number of mammals, represented by about 5,700 living species (excluding <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals">domesticated</a> species).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology_and_physiology">Morphology and physiology<span class="anchor" id="Systems"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Morphology and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation">Circulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Circulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_varano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="404" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg/220px-Wiki_varano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg/330px-Wiki_varano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_varano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermographic image of <a href="/wiki/Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard">monitor lizards</a></div></div></div>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Lepidosauria" title="Lepidosauria">lepidosaurs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a> have a three-chambered <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> consisting of two <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">atria</a>, one variably partitioned <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricle</a>, and two aortas that lead to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systemic_circulation" title="Systemic circulation">systemic circulation</a>. The degree of mixing of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygenated</a> and deoxygenated blood in the three-chambered heart varies depending on the species and physiological state. Under different conditions, deoxygenated blood can be shunted back to the body or oxygenated blood can be shunted back to the lungs. This variation in blood flow has been hypothesized to allow more effective thermoregulation and longer diving times for aquatic species, but has not been shown to be a <a href="/wiki/Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)">fitness</a> advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="838" data-file-width="748" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/220px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/330px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/440px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juvenile <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">Iguana</a> <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> bisected through the ventricle, bisecting the left and right atrium</div></div></div>
<p>For example, <a href="/wiki/Iguana" title="Iguana">Iguana</a> hearts, like the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamates</a> hearts, are composed of three chambers with two aorta and one ventricle, cardiac involuntary muscles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> The main structures of the heart are the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_venosus" title="Sinus venosus">sinus venosus</a>, the pacemaker, the <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">left atrium</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)">right atrium</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">atrioventricular valve</a>, the cavum venosum, cavum arteriosum, the cavum pulmonale, the muscular ridge, the ventricular ridge, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein">pulmonary veins</a>, and paired <a href="/wiki/Aortic_arches" title="Aortic arches">aortic arches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some squamate species (e.g., pythons and monitor lizards) have three-chambered hearts that become functionally four-chambered hearts during contraction. This is made possible by a muscular ridge that subdivides the ventricle during <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_cycle" title="Cardiac cycle">ventricular diastole</a> and completely divides it during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systole_(medicine)" title="Systole (medicine)">ventricular systole</a>. Because of this ridge, some of these <a href="/wiki/Squamata" title="Squamata">squamates</a> are capable of producing ventricular pressure differentials that are equivalent to those seen in mammalian and avian hearts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">Crocodilians</a> have an anatomically four-chambered heart, similar to <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, but also have two systemic aortas and are therefore capable of bypassing their <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/220px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/330px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/440px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sustained energy output (<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a>) of a typical reptile versus a similar size mammal as a function of core body temperature. The mammal has a much higher peak output, but can only function over a very narrow range of body temperature.</div></div></div>
<p>Modern non-avian reptiles exhibit some form of <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">cold-bloodedness</a> (i.e. some mix of <a href="/wiki/Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm">poikilothermy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm">ectothermy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bradymetabolism" title="Bradymetabolism">bradymetabolism</a>) so that they have limited physiological means of keeping the body temperature constant and often rely on external sources of heat. Due to a less stable core temperature than <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, reptilian biochemistry requires <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> capable of maintaining efficiency over a greater range of temperatures than in the case for <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-blooded</a> animals. The optimum body temperature range varies with species, but is typically below that of warm-blooded animals; for many lizards, it falls in the 24°–35 °C (75°–95 °F) range,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> while extreme heat-adapted species, like the American <a href="/wiki/Desert_iguana" title="Desert iguana">desert iguana</a> <i>Dipsosaurus dorsalis</i>, can have optimal physiological temperatures in the mammalian range, between 35° and 40 °C (95° and 104 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> While the optimum temperature is often encountered when the animal is active, the low basal metabolism makes body temperature drop rapidly when the animal is inactive.
</p><p>As in all animals, reptilian muscle action produces heat. In large reptiles, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leatherback_turtle" title="Leatherback turtle">leatherback turtles</a>, the low surface-to-volume ratio allows this metabolically produced heat to keep the animals warmer than their environment even though they do not have a <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-blooded</a> metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> This form of homeothermy is called <a href="/wiki/Gigantothermy" title="Gigantothermy">gigantothermy</a>; it has been suggested as having been common in large <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and other extinct large-bodied reptiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The benefit of a low resting metabolism is that it requires far less fuel to sustain bodily functions. By using temperature variations in their surroundings, or by remaining cold when they do not need to move, reptiles can save considerable amounts of energy compared to endothermic animals of the same size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> A crocodile needs from a tenth to a fifth of the food necessary for a <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a> of the same weight and can live half a year without eating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garnett_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garnett-79">[78]</a></sup> Lower food requirements and adaptive metabolisms allow reptiles to dominate the animal life in regions where net <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calorie</a> availability is too low to sustain large-bodied mammals and birds.
</p><p>It is generally assumed that reptiles are unable to produce the sustained high energy output necessary for long distance chases or flying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Higher energetic capacity might have been responsible for the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded">warm-bloodedness</a> in birds and mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> However, investigation of correlations between active capacity and <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermophysiology</a> show a weak relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGF1_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGF1-82">[81]</a></sup> Most extant reptiles are carnivores with a sit-and-wait feeding strategy; whether reptiles are cold blooded due to their ecology  is not clear. Energetic studies on some reptiles have shown active capacities equal to or greater than similar sized warm-blooded animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_system">Respiratory system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Reptile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Respiratory system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="22" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_(Alligator_mississippiensis)_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv" height="147" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/220px--X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="140200" data-framerate="29.97" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="720" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
